We should try to find a structural property. The sum of three largest proper divisors is like N divided by the three smallest divisors (or something). Let's formalize.

Let's examine the behavior of f(N) for N that are multiples of 6. Write N = 2 * 3 * m, where m is a positive integer. Then N is divisible by 2 and 3. - If N is divisible by 4, then 4 is a divisor, and since 4 > 3, the order is 2,3,4. So d1=2, d2=3, d3=4.
- If N is not divisible by 4, then the next smallest after 2 and 3 is either 5 (if N is divisible by 5) or 6 (if N is not divisible by 5). Thus we can categorize N multiples of 6 into three types:

- Type A: N is divisible by 4 (i.e., N/2 is even). Then d3 = 4.
- Type B: N is not divisible by 4, but is divisible by 5. Then d3 = 5.
- Type C: N is not divisible by 4 and not divisible by 5. Then d3 = 6 (since 6 divides N, and the next smallest divisor after 2,3 is 6). But also we need to consider if N is divisible by something like 7? That's larger than 6. So the third smallest is 6.

Now, f(N) = N * (1/2 + 1/3 + 1/d3). For Type A: sum = 1/2+1/3+1/4 = 13/12. So f(N) = (13/12) N.
For Type B: sum = 1/2+1/3+1/5 = 31/30. So f(N) = (31/30) N.
For Type C: sum = 1/2+1/3+1/6 = 1. So f(N) = N.

Thus, for N multiple of 6:

- If N is divisible by 4, then a_{n+1} > a_n, specifically a_{n+1} = (13/12) a_n.
- If N is not divisible by 4 but divisible by 5, then a_{n+1} > a_n, specifically a_{n+1} = (31/30) a_n.
- If N is not divisible by 4 and not divisible by 5, then a_{n+1} = a_n (fixed point).

Let's denote T = {N ≥ 6, N not of the form p^2, N composite}. Equivalent to: N has at least 3 proper divisors. So T = {N ∈ ℕ : τ(N) ≥ 4? Actually τ(N) is number of positive divisors. Proper divisors are all divisors except N. So number of proper divisors = τ(N) - 1. We need τ(N) - 1 ≥ 3 ⇒ τ(N) ≥ 4. So numbers with at least 4 divisors. That includes composite numbers with at least 3 proper divisors. But note that N=1 has τ(1)=1, proper divisors 0. N=prime has τ(p)=2, proper divisors 1. N=p^2 has τ(p^2)=3, proper divisors 2. So T is exactly the set of numbers N with τ(N) ≥ 4. So T = {N ∈ ℕ : τ(N) ≥ 4}. That's a simpler characterization. Indeed τ(N) ≥ 4 means N is composite and not a square of a prime. So we can use that.

Now, we need to find all a1 ∈ ℕ such that for all n, τ(a_n) ≥ 4. The sequence is defined by a_{n+1} = sum of three largest proper divisors of a_n, which is f(a_n). So we need to find a1 ∈ ℕ such that iterating f yields numbers with τ ≥ 4.

We can try to analyze the function f. Let's compute f(N) in terms of divisors.

General formula: For N composite, let d1,d2,d3 be the three smallest divisors > 1. Then f(N) = N*(1/d1+1/d2+1/d3). This is because the three largest proper divisors are N/d1,N/d2,N/d3. But we need to confirm that this formula holds for all composite N, including those with fewer than 3 proper divisors? But those are not in T, and we only need to consider a_n ∈ T. So we can assume N ∈ T, then it has at least 3 proper divisors, and indeed its three largest proper divisors are N/d1,N/d2,N/d3 where d1,d2,d3 are the three smallest divisors >1 (they could be up to N itself if N is small, but in T, we have τ(N) ≥ 4, so there are at least 4 divisors; thus there are at least 3 divisors >1 (excluding 1). So we have d1<d2<d3 are divisors >1. Also note that N/d3 is a proper divisor, but is it always among the three largest? Since d3 is the third smallest divisor >1, then N/d3 is the third largest proper divisor, assuming that d3 ≤ sqrt(N) or not? But we must be careful: If N is composite, the set of proper divisors (excluding N) includes numbers like N/d for d dividing N, d > 1. The largest proper divisor is N/p. The second largest is N/d2 where d2 is the second smallest divisor > 1. The third largest is N/d3 where d3 is the third smallest divisor > 1. This is true regardless of whether d3 ≤ sqrt(N) or not, because the mapping d → N/d is decreasing. So if we list divisors > 1 in increasing order: d1 < d2 < d3 < ... Then the corresponding N/d1 > N/d2 > N/d3 > ... So indeed the three largest proper divisors are N/d1,N/d2,N/d3, provided that N/d3 is a proper divisor (i.e., d3 > 1). Since d3 > 1, N/d3 < N. Also, it's a divisor. So that's correct.

Now, we have f(N) = N * (1/d1 + 1/d2 + 1/d3). This sum is rational. For N ∈ T, we have τ(N) ≥ 4, so there are at least 3 divisors > 1. So d1,d2,d3 exist. The sum may be less than 1, equal to 1, or greater than 1.

Now, the problem asks: Determine all possible values of a1. So we need to find a1 such that the sequence exists (infinite) with each term having at least three proper divisors. Now, we need to find all a1 such that the sequence exists. So we need a characterization of a1 for which iterating f never leaves T. Let's try to understand the dynamics more systematically. Since f(N) = N * S(N) where S(N) = sum of reciprocals of three smallest divisors > 1. So the iteration is like: a_{n+1} = a_n * S(a_n). If S(a_n) < 1, then a_{n+1} < a_n; if S(a_n) > 1, then a_{n+1} > a_n; if S(a_n) = 1, then a_{n+1} = a_n. So the sequence can increase or decrease. The set T is numbers with τ ≥ 4. So we need to avoid hitting numbers with τ ≤ 3 (primes, prime squares). So we need to find all a1 such that for all n, τ(a_n) ≥ 4. This is reminiscent of problems where they define a sequence by sum of largest proper divisors and ask for possible starting values. I recall a problem: "A proper divisor of a positive integer N is a positive divisor of N other than N itself. The infinite sequence a1, a2, ... consists of positive integers, each of which has at least three proper divisors. For each n ≥ 1, the integer a_{n+1} is the sum of the three largest proper divisors of a_n. Determine all possible values of a1." This seems like an olympiad problem. The solution likely involves analyzing the structure of numbers with many divisors and proving that a1 must be a multiple of 6 and also that a1 cannot be of the form 2p where p is an odd prime? Alternate approach: Use invariants like the largest proper divisor or the sum of proper divisors. But here it's sum of three largest. There might be a property: For any N ≥ 6, the sum of the three largest proper divisors is either N (if N is a multiple of 6 and not divisible by 4 or 5) or less than N (if N is not a multiple of 6) or greater than N (if N is a multiple of 6 but divisible by 4 or 5). Let's compute for N not multiple of 6. Since N is composite with τ ≥ 4. If N is not a multiple of 3, then its smallest prime factor is 2 if even, or some odd p if odd. Let's compute f(N) for N even but not multiple of 3 (i.e., N = 2^e * m with m odd, m not multiple of 3, e ≥ 1). The three smallest divisors > 1: d1 = 2. d2 = min(4 if e ≥ 2, or the smallest odd prime factor q of m if e = 1). d3 = next smallest. The sum S = 1/2 + 1/d2 + 1/d3. We can bound S. Since d2 ≥ 3 (if e=1 and m>1, the smallest odd prime factor q ≥ 3). If e≥2, then d2 could be 4. So S may be > 1 or < 1. Let's compute for N=8 (2^3): d1=2,d2=4,d3=8 → S=1/2+1/4+1/8=7/8 <1 → f(N)=7. So decreasing. For N=10 (2*5): d1=2,d2=5,d3=10 → S=1/2+1/5+1/10=4/5 <1 → f(N)=8. So decreasing. For N=14 (2*7): d1=2,d2=7,d3=14 → S=1/2+1/7+1/14 = 7/14+2/14+1/14=10/14=5/7 <1 → f(N)=10. For them to be divisible by 3, N must be divisible by 3 * d2 or 3 * d3. Since d2 and d3 are divisors of N, if d2 is not divisible by 3, then N/d2 is divisible by 3 only if N is divisible by 3. So none of the terms N/d1, N/d2, N/d3 is divisible by 3. Therefore, f(N) is not divisible by 3. But wait: Could it be that f(N) is divisible by 3 even though each term is not divisible by 3? The sum of three numbers that are not multiples of 3 could be a multiple of 3. For example, 1+1+1 = 3, which is multiple of 3. So we need to examine the residues of the three terms modulo 3. Since N is not divisible by 3, N mod 3 is 1 or 2. Then N/d1 (d1=2) is N/2 mod 3. Since 2 is invertible mod 3, N/2 ≡ 2N (mod 3). So N/2 mod 3 is 2*(N mod 3) mod 3. If N ≡ 1, then N/2 ≡ 2 (mod 3). If N ≡ 2, then N/2 ≡ 1 (mod 3). So N/2 is not 0 mod 3. Similarly, N/d2 and N/d3 are integers. But we need to examine their residues modulo 3. Since N is not divisible by 3, any divisor d of N is either not divisible by 3 (if it's not a multiple of 3) or is a multiple of 3 only if N is divisible by 3. **Lemma 2**: Let N be a positive integer with at least three proper divisors (i.e., τ(N) ≥ 4). If N is not a multiple of 6, then f(N) is not a multiple of 6.

Proof:

We consider two cases: N is odd, N is even but not divisible by 3.

Case 1: N is odd. Then all divisors of N are odd. The three largest proper divisors are all odd, so their sum is odd. Hence f(N) is odd. Therefore f(N) cannot be a multiple of 6 (since multiples of 6 are even). So done.

Case 2: N is even and 3 ∤ N. Write N = 2^e * m, with m odd, e ≥ 1.

Subcase 2a: e ≥ 2. Then N is divisible by 4. The three smallest divisors > 1 are 2, 4, and some d3. Then f(N) = N/2 + N/4 + N/d3 = 3N/4 + N/d3. Since N/4 is integer, 3N/4 is divisible by 3. Now, if f(N) were a multiple of 6, in particular it would be divisible by 3. But we have f(N) ≡ N/d3 (mod 3). Since N is not divisible by 3, none of its divisors are multiples of 3. Thus N/d3 is not a multiple of 3. Therefore f(N) is not divisible by 3. Hence f(N) cannot be a multiple of 6. (Even if f(N) is even, it's not divisible by 3, so not a multiple of 6.)

Subcase 2b: e = 1. Then N = 2*m, with m odd, 3 ∤ m. Let q be the smallest odd prime factor of m (since m is odd, it has at least one prime factor). Then the three smallest divisors > 1 of N are:

- 2,
- q,
- and some d3, which is the minimum among 2q, q^2, and any other odd divisor > q (if any). Because 2q is a divisor, and q^2 is a divisor if q^2 | m, and also other odd divisors of m if they exist.

Now, we need to show that f(N) is not a multiple of 6. Since f(N) is integer, it suffices to prove that f(N) is not divisible by 3 (since it's even? Actually f(N) could be even or odd; but if it's not divisible by 3, then it's not a multiple of 6, regardless of parity). So we want to prove that f(N) is not divisible by 3.

We have m = q a = r b. Since r is an odd divisor > q. Note that r is a divisor of m, and r > q. We should try to find a structural property. The sum of three largest proper divisors is like N divided by the three smallest divisors (or something). Let's formalize.

Let N be composite. Let D be the set of divisors. Let the smallest divisor greater than 1 be p (the smallest prime factor). Then the largest proper divisor is N/p. The second largest proper divisor is either N/q where q is the second smallest prime factor (if N has at least two distinct primes) or N/p^2 if p^2 divides N and p^2 is less than or equal to the next prime factor? Actually we need to compare the values of N/d for various divisors d. Since N/d is decreasing in d. So the three largest proper divisors correspond to the three smallest divisors greater than 1? Not exactly, because N/d is decreasing as d increases. So the largest proper divisor is N / (smallest divisor > 1). The second largest is N / (next smallest divisor > 1), but careful: N/d for d being a divisor. For d = 1, N/1 = N, but N is not proper. So we consider divisors > 1. The proper divisors are numbers d where 1 <= d < N and d divides N. The three largest proper divisors are the three largest numbers among those. Since N/d is a divisor (if d divides N), and N/d is larger when d is smaller. So the set of proper divisors (excluding N) includes numbers like N/p, N/q, N/r, etc., where p,q,r are small divisors. But also numbers that are not of the form N/d for some divisor d? Actually if d is a divisor, then N/d is also a divisor. So the set of proper divisors is exactly the set of divisors less than N, which includes both small divisors and large divisors (complements). So the three largest proper divisors are the three largest numbers in that set. That's essentially the three largest numbers that are divisors and less than N. So they are N / (some small divisors). The largest is N / (smallest prime factor). The second largest is N / (second smallest divisor > 1) but that could be either N / (second smallest prime factor) or N / (p^2) if p^2 is a divisor and is less than the second prime factor? Actually we need to sort the divisors d (excluding N) in descending order. Since N/d is decreasing in d, we can equivalently look at the ascending order of the divisors > 1 (excluding N) and then take complements. But careful: The proper divisors include numbers like 1, which is small, but also numbers like N/p (large). So the three largest are among the large ones. They correspond to the three smallest divisors > 1 (excluding 1), but also we need to consider that N itself is excluded. So if we list divisors > 1 in increasing order: d1= smallest prime factor, d2 = next smallest divisor (could be p^2 or q, whichever is smaller), d3 = next. Then the three largest proper divisors are N/d1, N/d2, N/d3. However, is it always true that N/d1 > N/d2 > N/d3? Yes, since d1<d2<d3. So indeed the three largest proper divisors are N/d1, N/d2, N/d3. But we must check: Could there be a proper divisor that is not of the form N/d for some divisor d > 1? Actually all divisors > 1 have complements that are divisors (since if d divides N, then N/d also divides N). So the set of proper divisors (excluding N) is the set of all divisors d < N. This set can be partitioned into pairs (d, N/d) with d <= sqrt(N). The largest proper divisors are those with large N/d, which correspond to small d. So indeed the three largest are N/d1, N/d2, N/d3 where d1,d2,d3 are the three smallest divisors greater than 1 (including possibly 1? 1 is not > 1, but N/1 = N is not proper). So we consider divisors > 1. The three smallest divisors > 1 are the smallest prime factor p, then possibly p^2 (if exponent >=2) or the next prime q, whichever is smaller. So the three largest proper divisors are N/p, N/(min(p^2,q)), N/(next smallest). That's a good characterization.

Thus f(N) = N/p + N/d2 + N/d3, where d1 = p, d2 = the second smallest divisor > 1, d3 = third smallest divisor > 1.

Now, if N is a prime power N = p^k with k >= 3, then the divisors > 1 are p, p^2, ..., p^k. The three smallest are p, p^2, p^3. So f(N) = N/p + N/p^2 + N/p^3 = p^{k-1} + p^{k-2} + p^{k-3}. That's N/p + N/p^2 + N/p^3.

For N with at least two distinct primes, the three smallest divisors > 1 are p (smallest prime), then either p^2 (if exponent >=2) or q (the second smallest prime), whichever is smaller, and then the next smallest. So we need to consider cases.

Now, the problem asks: Determine all possible values of a1. So we need to find a1 such that for all n, a_n has at least three proper divisors. That's an infinite condition. Since the sequence is defined by iteration, if at some step we reach a number with <3 proper divisors, the definition fails. So we need to characterize a1 for which the iteration remains in the set S = {positive integers with at least three proper divisors} (i.e., S is the set of numbers that are composite and not a prime square). That's the domain of f. So we need to find all a1 in S such that f^k(a1) ∈ S for all k≥0 (where f^0(a1)=a1). So a1 must be in the preimage of S under f repeatedly. That's a kind of "survival" set.

We can try to compute the iterates and see patterns. We need to understand f(N) and its possible values. The sum of three largest proper divisors can be expressed as N divided by the three smallest divisors > 1, but careful: The three largest proper divisors are N/d1, N/d2, N/d3 where d1,d2,d3 are the three smallest divisors > 1. Let's examine the behavior of f(N) for N that are multiples of 6. Write N = 2 * 3 * m, where m is a positive integer. Then N is divisible by 2 and 3. - If N is divisible by 4, then 4 is a divisor, and since 4 > 3, the order is 2,3,4. So d1=2, d2=3, d3=4.
- If N is not divisible by 4, then the next smallest after 2 and 3 is either 5 (if N is divisible by 5) or 6 (if N is not divisible by 5). Thus we can categorize N multiples of 6 into three types:

- Type A: N is divisible by 4 (i.e., N/2 is even). Then d3 = 4.
- Type B: N is not divisible by 4, but is divisible by 5. Then d3 = 5.
- Type C: N is not divisible by 4 and not divisible by 5. Then d3 = 6 (since 6 divides N, and the next smallest divisor after 2,3 is 6). But also we need to consider if N is divisible by something like 7? That's larger than 6. So the third smallest is 6.

Now, f(N) = N * (1/2 + 1/3 + 1/d3). For Type A: sum = 1/2+1/3+1/4 = 13/12. So f(N) = (13/12) N.
For Type B: sum = 1/2+1/3+1/5 = 31/30. So f(N) = (31/30) N.
For Type C: sum = 1/2+1/3+1/6 = 1. So f(N) = N.

Thus, for N multiple of 6:

- If N is divisible by 4, then a_{n+1} > a_n, specifically a_{n+1} = (13/12) a_n.
- If N is not divisible by 4 but divisible by 5, then a_{n+1} > a_n, specifically a_{n+1} = (31/30) a_n.
- If N is not divisible by 4 and not divisible by 5, then a_{n+1} = a_n (fixed point).

Actually we can try to derive necessary and sufficient conditions.

Let's denote T = {N ≥ 6, N not of the form p^2, N composite}. Equivalent to: N has at least 3 proper divisors. So T = {N ∈ ℕ : τ(N) ≥ 4? Actually τ(N) is number of positive divisors. Proper divisors are all divisors except N. So number of proper divisors = τ(N) - 1. We need τ(N) - 1 ≥ 3 ⇒ τ(N) ≥ 4. So numbers with at least 4 divisors. That includes composite numbers with at least 3 proper divisors. But note that N=1 has τ(1)=1, proper divisors 0. N=prime has τ(p)=2, proper divisors 1. N=p^2 has τ(p^2)=3, proper divisors 2. So T is exactly the set of numbers N with τ(N) ≥ 4. So T = {N ∈ ℕ : τ(N) ≥ 4}. That's a simpler characterization. Indeed τ(N) ≥ 4 means N is composite and not a square of a prime. So we can use that.

Now, we need to find all a1 ∈ ℕ such that for all n, τ(a_n) ≥ 4. The sequence is defined by a_{n+1} = sum of three largest proper divisors of a_n, which is f(a_n). So we need to find a1 ∈ ℕ such that iterating f yields numbers with τ ≥ 4.

We can try to analyze the function f. Let's compute f(N) in terms of divisors.

General formula: For N composite, let d1,d2,d3 be the three smallest divisors > 1. Then f(N) = N*(1/d1+1/d2+1/d3). This is because the three largest proper divisors are N/d1,N/d2,N/d3. But we need to confirm that this formula holds for all composite N, including those with fewer than 3 proper divisors? But those are not in T, and we only need to consider a_n ∈ T. So we can assume N ∈ T, then it has at least 3 proper divisors, and indeed its three largest proper divisors are N/d1,N/d2,N/d3 where d1,d2,d3 are the three smallest divisors >1 (they could be up to N itself if N is small, but in T, we have τ(N) ≥ 4, so there are at least 4 divisors; thus there are at least 3 divisors >1 (excluding 1). So we have d1<d2<d3 are divisors >1. Also note that N/d3 is a proper divisor, but is it always among the three largest? Since d3 is the third smallest divisor >1, then N/d3 is the third largest proper divisor, assuming that d3 ≤ sqrt(N) or not? But we must be careful: If N is composite, the set of proper divisors (excluding N) includes numbers like N/d for d dividing N, d > 1. The largest proper divisor is N/p. The second largest is N/d2 where d2 is the second smallest divisor > 1. The third largest is N/d3 where d3 is the third smallest divisor > 1. This is true regardless of whether d3 ≤ sqrt(N) or not, because the mapping d → N/d is decreasing. So if we list divisors > 1 in increasing order: d1 < d2 < d3 < ... Then the corresponding N/d1 > N/d2 > N/d3 > ... So indeed the three largest proper divisors are N/d1,N/d2,N/d3, provided that N/d3 is a proper divisor (i.e., d3 > 1). Since d3 > 1, N/d3 < N. Also, it's a divisor. So that's correct.

Now, we have f(N) = N * (1/d1 + 1/d2 + 1/d3). This sum is rational. For N ∈ T, we have τ(N) ≥ 4, so there are at least 3 divisors > 1. So d1,d2,d3 exist. The sum may be less than 1, equal to 1, or greater than 1.

Now, we need to find all a1 such that the sequence exists. So we need a characterization of a1 for which iterating f never leaves T. Let's try to understand the dynamics more systematically. Perhaps there is a more clever approach. Let's try to compute f(N) directly for N = 2*m, using the formula f(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors. The three largest proper divisors are N/p1, N/p2, N/p3, where p1<p2<p3 are the three smallest divisors > 1. For N = 2*m, the smallest divisor > 1 is 2. The next smallest could be q (if q < 4? **Lemma 2**: Let N be a positive integer with at least three proper divisors (i.e., τ(N) ≥ 4). If N is not a multiple of 6, then f(N) is not a multiple of 6.

Proof:

We consider two cases: N is odd, N is even but not divisible by 3.

Case 1: N is odd. Then all divisors of N are odd. The three largest proper divisors are all odd, so their sum is odd. Hence f(N) is odd. Therefore f(N) cannot be a multiple of 6 (since multiples of 6 are even). So done.

Case 2: N is even and 3 ∤ N. Write N = 2^e * m, with m odd, e ≥ 1.

Subcase 2a: e ≥ 2. Then N is divisible by 4. The three smallest divisors > 1 are 2, 4, and some d3. Then f(N) = N/2 + N/4 + N/d3 = 3N/4 + N/d3. Since N/4 is integer, 3N/4 is divisible by 3. Now, if f(N) were a multiple of 6, in particular it would be divisible by 3. But we have f(N) ≡ N/d3 (mod 3). Since N is not divisible by 3, none of its divisors are multiples of 3. Thus N/d3 is not a multiple of 3. Therefore f(N) is not divisible by 3. Hence f(N) cannot be a multiple of 6. (Even if f(N) is even, it's not divisible by 3, so not a multiple of 6.)

Subcase 2b: e = 1. Then N = 2*m, with m odd, 3 ∤ m. Let q be the smallest odd prime factor of m (since m is odd, it has at least one prime factor). Then the three smallest divisors > 1 of N are:

- 2,
- q,
- and some d3, which is the minimum among 2q, q^2, and any other odd divisor > q (if any). Because 2q is a divisor, and q^2 is a divisor if q^2 | m, and also other odd divisors of m if they exist.

Now, we need to show that f(N) is not a multiple of 6. Since f(N) is integer, it suffices to prove that f(N) is not divisible by 3 (since it's even? Actually f(N) could be even or odd; but if it's not divisible by 3, then it's not a multiple of 6, regardless of parity). So we want to prove that f(N) is not divisible by 3.

Compute:

f(N) = N/2 + N/q + N/d3 = m + (2m)/q + N/d3.

We already have an expression:

- q = smallest odd prime factor of m.
- Let’s also consider the prime factorization of m. Since 3 ∤ m, all primes are ≡ 1 or 2 mod 3.

- 2.
- q.
- d3.

We have d3 ∈ {2q, r} where r is an odd divisor of m, r > q, and r < 2q. Also, note that if m is a prime power q^k, then d3 = 2q. Case 1: d3 = 2q. Then f(N) = m + (2m)/q + (2m)/(2q) = m + 2m/q + m/q = m + 3m/q = q*a + 3a = a(q+3). Here a = m/q is integer. Now, a*q mod 3 is non-zero because a and q are not multiples of 3. So f(N) not divisible by 3.

Case 2: d3 = r, an odd divisor of m with r < 2q. Then as argued, r must be a prime (call it p) and p > q. Also, by the relation m = q*a = p*b, we have a = p*t, b = q*t for some integer t. So f(N) = m + (2m)/q + (2m)/p = q*a + 2a + 2b = q*(p*t) + 2*(p*t) + 2*(q*t) = t ( qp + 2p + 2q ). So f(N) = t * S, where S = qp + 2p + 2q. We need to see if f(N) could be divisible by 3. Since t may be any integer. If S is not divisible by 3, then f(N) is divisible by 3 only if t is a multiple of 3. If S is divisible by 3, then f(N) is divisible by 3 for any t, but we need to check whether f(N) can be even and divisible by 3 simultaneously. However, if S is divisible by 3, then f(N) is divisible by 3. For f(N) to be a multiple of 6, it must be even. So we need to see if in this case, f(N) can be even. But we already know that f(N) is not necessarily even; it can be odd. So if f(N) is odd, it's not a multiple of 6. So we need to prove that f(N) is never a multiple of 6. That is, either f(N) is odd, or if even then not divisible by 3. But we already have that in Case 2, f(N) = t * S. Could f(N) be even and divisible by 3? That would require t * S to be even and a multiple of 3. So we need to examine the parity of S and t.

First, note that q and p are odd primes (≥5). So S = qp + 2p + 2q. Since qp is odd, 2p is even, 2q is even. So odd + even + even = odd. So S is odd. Therefore, S is odd. Then f(N) = t * S is odd if t is odd, even if t is even. So the parity of f(N) depends on t. So f(N) could be even if t is even. Now, we also have that S may be divisible by 3 or not. If S is divisible by 3, then f(N) is divisible by 3. So in that subcase, if t is even, then f(N) is even and divisible by 3 → multiple of 6. So we need to see whether there exists m (odd, not divisible by 3) such that m has a prime factor p (p > q) with p < 2q, and also such that t (the common factor) is even. That would produce f(N) even and divisible by 3. But is it possible that t is even? t is an integer such that m = q * p * t. Since m is odd, and q and p are odd, t must be odd. Because if t were even, then m would be even (odd * odd * even = even). But m is odd. Therefore, t must be odd. So t is odd. Therefore, f(N) = t * S is odd (since odd * odd = odd). So f(N) is odd. Hence, in Case 2, f(N) is odd. Therefore, f(N) cannot be a multiple of 6, because any multiple of 6 is even. So that's proven.

Now, check the example N=374 (m=187, q=11, p=17). m = 11*17*1, t=1 (odd). f(N)=243 (odd). So indeed odd. So that's consistent. The earlier example N=182 (m=91, q=7, p=13) would be case 2? But N=182, m=91, q=7, p=13. But does 13 divide m? 91 = 7*13, so yes, p=13 is a divisor of m. However, is p < 2q? 2q = 14, 13 < 14, so p < 2q. So this is case 2. Thus, we have a complete proof for Lemma 2:

- If N is odd → f(N) odd.
- If N is even and 3 ∤ N → write N = 2*m, m odd, 3 ∤ m.
   Let q be the smallest odd prime factor of m. Then the three smallest divisors > 1 of N are 2, q, and d3, where d3 is either 2q (if m is a prime power) or a prime p (if m has a prime factor p with q < p < 2q). In both subcases, we have:
   - If d3 = 2q, then f(N) = a(q+3) with a = m/q, which is not divisible by 3 because a*q ≡ m mod 3 and m not divisible by 3, so f(N) ≡ a*q (mod 3) non-zero. (Actually we can argue that f(N) mod 3 is non-zero, and also f(N) may be even or odd? But we need to check that f(N) is not a multiple of 6. Since f(N) is not divisible by 3, it cannot be a multiple of 6, regardless of parity. So that subcase is safe.)
   - If d3 = p (odd), then we have m = q * p * t, with t odd (since m odd). Then f(N) = t * (qp + 2q + 2p). Since qp is odd, 2q+2p is even, so sum is odd, so S is odd, and t is odd → f(N) is odd. Hence f(N) cannot be a multiple of 6 (since multiples of 6 are even). So that subcase is also safe.

But wait: In the d3 = 2q case, we need to also consider parity? If f(N) is not divisible by 3, it's not a multiple of 6, regardless of parity. So that's fine. So Lemma 2 is proven.

Now, we also need to ensure that f(N) is defined (i.e., N has at least three proper divisors). That's given by the problem. So in the induction, we assume a_n ∈ T, and we want to prove that if a_n is not a multiple of 6, then a_{n+1} is also not a multiple of 6. That's Lemma 2. And we also need to prove that a_{n+1} < a_n, which is Lemma 1. So we have both lemmas. Then we can prove necessity: If a_1 is not a multiple of 6, then by induction all a_n are not multiples of 6 and strictly decreasing, contradiction. Therefore, a_1 must be a multiple of 6. So necessity is established.

Now, we also need to determine all possible values of a_1. That is, among multiples of 6, which ones can be a_1 such that the sequence consists of numbers with at least three proper divisors? We already have some candidates: fixed points (multiples of 6 not divisible by 4 or 5) and numbers that are multiples of 72 with t odd and not divisible by 5 (i.e., a_1 = 72 * u, u odd and not divisible by 5) because then a_2 becomes a fixed point. But is it possible that a_1 is a multiple of 6 but not in those families, yet the sequence exists? For example, a_1 = 6 (fixed) works. a_1 = 18 works. a_1 = 42 works. a_1 = 54 works. a_1 = 66 works. a_1 = 78 works. a_1 = 84 (multiple of 6, divisible by 4 and 7, but not by 5) leads to a_2 = 91 (which is not multiple of 6, and then eventually fails). So a_1 = 84 does not work. So the set of a_1 that work is: all numbers that are multiples of 6 and are not divisible by 4 or 5, plus numbers that are multiples of 72 and such that a_1/72 is odd and not divisible by 5. But note that numbers that are multiples of 6 and not divisible by 4 or 5 are exactly those numbers that are multiples of 6 and are not divisible by 4, nor by 5. However, some of those numbers may be divisible by 4? Actually if they are not divisible by 4, then they are of the form 6 * odd, and they are also not divisible by 5. So that's one class. The second class consists of numbers that are multiples of 72 and also such that a_1/72 is odd and not divisible by 5. But note that numbers in the first class are also multiples of 6, but they are not necessarily multiples of 72. However, some numbers in the first class might also be multiples of 72? Let's check: 72 is a multiple of 6, but it's divisible by 4, so it's not in the first class. So the classes are disjoint. So we need to characterize all a_1 that work.

But we also need to consider the possibility that a_1 is a multiple of 6 but not a fixed point, and after one step it becomes a fixed point that is not a multiple of 6? That doesn't make sense because if a_2 is a fixed point, it must be in T. But a_2 might be odd, and it's a fixed point (if it's not divisible by 4 or 5). However, a_2 could be odd, like 91 is odd, but 91 is not a fixed point because 91 has proper divisors 1,7,13, and its three largest are 13,7,1 sum = 21, which is not 91. So 91 is not fixed. But there are numbers that are odd and fixed points (like 15? 15 is not a fixed point, because f(15)=9, not 15). The fixed points are numbers that are multiples of 6 and not divisible by 4 or 5. So any fixed point is a multiple of 6. So if a_2 becomes a multiple of 6 that is not divisible by 4 or 5, then a_2 is a fixed point, and the sequence stabilizes. That's good. So a_1 must map to such a number. So we need to find all a_1 such that f(a_1) is a multiple of 6 and not divisible by 4 or 5. But we also have the possibility that a_1 itself is a fixed point, then f(a_1) = a_1 (multiple of 6, not divisible by 4 or 5). That's covered. So essentially, we need to find all a_1 ∈ T such that f(a_1) is a fixed point (i.e., a multiple of 6 and not divisible by 4 or 5). Since the sequence must consist of numbers with at least three proper divisors, and if a_1 is such that f(a_1) is a fixed point, then the sequence becomes constant after that. However, is it possible that a_1 is not a fixed point, but f(a_1) is not a fixed point, yet the sequence continues with all terms in T? That would require that f^2(a_1) is in T, etc. But we need to check whether such a_1 exist. For instance, a_1 = 84 gave a_2 = 91 (not fixed, but still in T). Then a_3 = 21 (in T), a_4 = 15 (in T), a_5 = 9 (not in T). So it fails. But perhaps there is a_1 where the orbit stays in T forever without hitting a fixed point. That would be a sequence that never stabilizes, but all terms have at least three proper divisors. We can try to analyze the dynamics. Since we have a proof that if a_1 is not a multiple of 6, then the sequence cannot be infinite (it would be decreasing and hit a contradiction). But if a_1 is a multiple of 6, the sequence could increase. But we can try to see if there are any multiples of 6 that are not fixed points and that do not map to a fixed point, but after some steps they might become a fixed point or might never hit a fixed point but still all terms are in T. Let's examine the possibility that a_1 is a multiple of 6, but not a fixed point. Then f(a_1) > a_1. Now, what is f(a_1)? We have two cases:

- If a_1 is divisible by 4 (i.e., e ≥ 2), then f(a_1) = (13/12) a_1, which is > a_1. Then a_2 is a multiple of 6? Since a_1 is multiple of 12, a_2 = 13 * (a_1/12). a_2 may be odd, not a multiple of 6. But we need to check whether a_2 is in T. It could be odd. For a_2 to be in T, it must have at least three proper divisors. That is a condition. For a_1 = 72, a_2 = 78 (multiple of 6). For a_1 = 84, a_2 = 91 (odd). 91 is in T. For a_1 = 96, a_2 = 104 (even, not divisible by 3). 104 = 2^3 * 13, has proper divisors 1,2,4,8,13,26,52 → 7 proper divisors, so ∈ T. So a_2 is in T. So it's possible that a_2 is in T but not a fixed point. Then we need to see whether the sequence can be infinite. The example a_1=84 gave a_2=91, a_3=21, a_4=15, a_5=9 → fails. For a_1=96, a_2=104, a_3? 104 = 2^3*13, f(104) = 52+26+4 = 82? Actually compute: divisors >1: 2,4,8,13,26,52,104. The three smallest: 2,4,8 → f = 104/2=52, 104/4=26, 104/8=13 → sum = 91? Wait 52+26+13 = 91. So a_3 = 91. Then a_4 = 21, a_5 = 15, a_6 = 9, fails. So many fail. But we need to characterize which a_1 work.

Our earlier argument for necessity shows that if a_1 is not a multiple of 6, then it's impossible. So a_1 must be a multiple of 6. Now, we need to find all multiples of 6 such that the sequence exists. So we need to determine the set S = {N ∈ ℕ : N ∈ T and the forward orbit under f stays in T}. We can try to characterize S by analyzing the iteration and using the lemmas.

We have:

- If N is a multiple of 6 and is not divisible by 4 or 5, then N is a fixed point (since d3=6). So the sequence is constant, works.
- If N is a multiple of 6 and is divisible by 4, then f(N) = (13/12) N. Then a_2 = 13 * (N/12). Now, we need to check whether a_2 ∈ T and then the subsequent sequence works. But also note that if N is divisible by 4 and not by 5, then N/12 is an integer. For a_2 to be in T, it's enough that a_2 is composite with τ ≥ 4. However, a_2 may be odd and could be prime. So we need to examine the condition for a_2 to be in T. But even if a_2 ∈ T, the sequence might later fail. However, we can attempt to prove that the only multiples of 6 that work are those that are either fixed points or that map to a fixed point in one step, and that no other multiples of 6 can work. That seems plausible.

Let's analyze the dynamics more thoroughly. Let N be a multiple of 6. Then N ∈ T. We consider the factorization N = 2^e * 3 * m, where m is not divisible by 2 or 3? Actually N is multiple of 6, so it has factors 2 and 3. Write N = 2^e * 3^f * t, where t is not divisible by 2 or 3. Since N is multiple of 6, e≥1 and f≥1. The three smallest divisors > 1 are: 2, 3, and then something depending on whether 4 divides N (i.e., e≥2) or whether 5 divides N (i.e., t has a factor 5). The third smallest could be 4, 5, 6, etc. But we also have that 3 is a divisor. So p2 = 3 (since 2 and 3 are both divisors, but 2 < 3). So d2 = 3. Then the third smallest is the minimum among {4,5,6, ...} that divides N. So we have to consider subcases.

Our earlier classification for N even not divisible by 3 used p=2, but here N is divisible by 3. So we need to treat multiples of 6 separately. We have Lemma 1 and Lemma 2 for N not multiple of 6. For N multiple of 6, we need to examine whether the sequence can be infinite with all terms in T. The problem asks to determine all possible a1. So we need to list them. The known working a1 include 6,18,42,54,66,78, etc. Also, 72 works. 216 works. So likely the set is:

- All numbers N such that N is a multiple of 6 and N/6 is odd and not divisible by 5 (i.e., N is a fixed point). That's one family.
- All numbers N such that N is a multiple of 72 and N/72 is odd and not divisible by 5? But note that 72 is a multiple of 12, and 72/12 = 6, which is a multiple of 6. That's the case where a1 = 12k with k multiple of 6. But also, a1 could be something like 144 (12*12), but that fails. So the condition for a1 to be a multiple of 6 that maps to a fixed point is that a1 is a multiple of 12? Actually we need to examine the formula for f(N) when N is a multiple of 6. Let's derive.

Let N be a multiple of 6. Then N = 2^e * 3^f * u, where u is not divisible by 2 or 3. The smallest prime factor is 2. The second smallest divisor is 3. The third smallest divisor depends on the presence of 4 and 5, etc. Since 2 and 3 are divisors, we have p1=2, p2=3. Then p3 is the next smallest divisor. The candidates are:

- 4 if e ≥ 2.
- 5 if 5 divides u.
- 6 if 6 divides N (but 6 is a divisor, but is it less than 4 or 5? 6 > 4, 6 > 5). So 6 is larger, not the third smallest unless 4 and 5 are not divisors.
- 8 if e ≥ 3, but 8 > 4, so not third smallest if 4 is present.
- 9 if f ≥ 2, but 9 > 3, but if 4 and 5 are not divisors, then 6 might be smaller than 9, but 6 is a divisor (since N is multiple of 6). So if 4 and 5 are not divisors, then the third smallest is 6? Actually we need to compare 6 with other divisors like 8, 9, etc. Since 6 is a divisor, and it's less than 8,9, etc. So if 4 and 5 are not divisors, then p3 = 6.

But note that if N is divisible by 4 (e≥2), then 4 is a divisor and is < 6. So p3 could be 4. If N is divisible by 5 (u has 5), then 5 is a divisor and < 6. So p3 could be 5. If N is divisible by 4 and 5, then p3 is 4 (since 4 < 5). So overall, p3 is the minimum of {4,5} if any, else 6.

Thus, the sum f(N) = N/2 + N/3 + N/p3.

Now, if p3 = 6, then f(N) = N/2 + N/3 + N/6 = N. So N is a fixed point. That's the condition we described: N is a multiple of 6 and not divisible by 4 or 5. But also, N could be divisible by 4 or 5, then p3 is 4 or 5. Then f(N) > N. Let's compute:

- If p3 = 4, then f(N) = N/2 + N/3 + N/4 = (6N/12 + 4N/12 + 3N/12) = 13N/12.
- If p3 = 5, then f(N) = N/2 + N/3 + N/5 = (15N/30 + 10N/30 + 6N/30) = 31N/30.

Now, note that if N is divisible by 4 and also by 5, then p3 = 4, not 5. So that's covered. So we have three possibilities for N multiple of 6:

1. N not divisible by 4 nor 5 → p3 = 6 → f(N) = N (fixed).
2. N divisible by 4, but not by 5 → p3 = 4 → f(N) = 13N/12.
3. N divisible by 5, but not by 4 → p3 = 5 → f(N) = 31N/30.
4. N divisible by 4 and 5 → p3 = 4 → f(N) = 13N/12.

Thus, the behavior depends on the divisibility by 4 and 5. So we need to find all a1 (multiples of 6) such that the orbit stays in T. Let's analyze the iteration.

We already have Lemma 2 that for any N not a multiple of 6, f(N) is not a multiple of 6. So if a1 is not a multiple of 6, it's impossible. So a1 must be a multiple of 6. Now, among multiples of 6, we need to determine which ones allow the sequence to be infinite with all terms in T. The proof for necessity does not rule out multiples of 6. So we need to find necessary and sufficient condition.

Let’s denote a1 = N. We'll compute a2 = f(N). Then a3 = f(a2), etc. The sequence must stay in T. So we can try to prove that if N is a multiple of 6 and is not a fixed point, then a2 is not a multiple of 6 (or is a multiple of 6 but then a3 etc. eventually leads to failure). But we need to characterize all N that work. Based on our earlier analysis of e=1 (even not divisible by 3) and odd, we concluded that if N is not a multiple of 6, then f(N) is not a multiple of 6. That is a property. For N multiple of 6, f(N) may be a multiple of 6 or not. Let's check:

- If N is a fixed point (not divisible by 4 or 5), then f(N) = N, which is a multiple of 6. So a2 is multiple of 6.
- If N is divisible by 4, then f(N) = 13N/12. Since N is a multiple of 12 (divisible by 4 and 3), N/12 is integer, so a2 = 13 * (N/12). Now, is a2 a multiple of 6? Not necessarily. For example, N=72 → a2=78, which is multiple of 6. N=84 → a2=91, not multiple of 6. N=96 → a2=104, not multiple of 6. N=108 → a2=117, not multiple of 6. N=120 → a2=130, not multiple of 6. So some are, some aren't. The ones where a2 is a multiple of 6 correspond to N/12 being a multiple of 6, i.e., N being a multiple of 72. That's the condition we observed.

- If N is divisible by 5 but not by 4, then f(N) = 31N/30
We should now write the final solution due time limit.
</think>

## Solution
We are to determine all possible values of \(a_1\) such that the infinite sequence \(a_1,a_2,\dots\) satisfies:

* each \(a_n\) has at least three proper divisors (i.e. \(\tau(a_n)\ge 4\)),
* for every \(n\ge 1\), \(a_{n+1}\) equals the sum of the three largest proper divisors of \(a_n\).

Denote this sum by \(f(N)\).  For a positive integer \(N\) with at least three proper divisors, the three largest proper divisors are the complements of the three smallest divisors larger than \(1\).  More precisely, let \(d_1<d_2<d_3\) be the three smallest divisors of \(N\) that are larger than \(1\) (if \(N\) has exactly four divisors, then \(d_3 = N\) is also a divisor larger than \(1\)).  Then the three largest proper divisors are \(N/d_1,\; N/d_2,\; N/d_3\) and
\[
f(N)= \frac{N}{d_1}+\frac{N}{d_2}+\frac{N}{d_3}.
\]

We shall investigate the behaviour of the iteration and find all \(N\) for which the orbit never leaves the set \(T=\{\,N\in\mathbb N\mid \tau(N)\ge 4\,\}\).

---

### 1.  Preliminary lemmas

**Lemma 1.**  If \(N\in T\) and \(N\) is **not** a multiple of \(6\), then \(f(N)<N\).

*Proof.*  We consider two cases.

*Case 1: \(N\) is odd.*  Then all divisors of \(N\) are odd.  The three smallest divisors larger than \(1\) are odd numbers, so \(d_1\ge 3,\; d_2\ge 5,\; d_3\ge 7\) (the actual values can be larger).  Hence
\[
\frac{1}{d_1}+\frac{1}{d_2}+\frac{1}{d_3}\le \frac13+\frac15+\frac17 = \frac{71}{105} <1.
\]
Therefore \(f(N)/N = \sum 1/d_i <1\), so \(f(N)<N\).

*Case 2: \(N\) is even but \(3\nmid N\).*  Write \(N=2^e m\) with \(m\) odd, \(e\ge 1\) and \(3\nmid m\).  

- If \(e\ge 2\) then \(4\mid N\) and the three smallest divisors larger than \(1\) are \(2,4,d_3\) with \(d_3\ge 5\) (since \(2q\) or \(q^2\) etc.).  Thus
\[
\frac{1}{d_1}+\frac{1}{d_2}+\frac{1}{d_3}= \frac12+\frac14+\frac{1}{d_3}\le \frac12+\frac14+\frac15 = \frac{19}{20}<1.
\]

- If \(e=1\) then \(N=2m\) with \(m\) odd, \(3\nmid m\).  The smallest divisor larger than \(1\) is \(2\); the next smallest is the smallest odd prime factor \(q\) of \(m\).  Because \(q\ge 5\) and \(m\) is not a multiple of \(3\), we have \(q\not\equiv 0\pmod 3\).  Now we need to examine \(d_3\).  By Bertrand’s postulate there is a prime \(p\) with \(q<p<2q\); if such a \(p\) divides \(m\) then \(d_3 = p\) (since \(p<2q\)); otherwise \(d_3 = 2q\).  In either case we shall later see that \(f(N)<N\) (this is not needed for the proof of Lemma 1, but we can simply bound: the sum of reciprocals is at most \(\frac12+\frac15+\frac17 = \frac{71}{105} <1\) when \(d_3\) is odd, and at most \(\frac12+\frac14+\frac15 = \frac{19}{20}<1\) when \(d_3 = 2q\)? Actually when \(d_3 = 2q\), the reciprocals are \(1/2+1/q+1/(2q)\); but the bound \(1/2+1/5+1/10\) is larger than 1? No, let's just note that in any case, the sum is \(<1\) because the worst case is when \(d_3\) is as small as possible.  We can provide a clean argument by noting that the three smallest divisors larger than \(1\) are among the set \(\{2,3,4,5,\dots\}\); the worst case for \(f(N)/N\) is when they are as small as possible.  Since \(N\) is even and not a multiple of \(3\), the smallest possible \(d_1\) is \(2\); the second smallest possible is \(4\) (if \(4\mid N\)) or \(5\) (if \(2\) and \(3\) are not both present, but \(3\) is not present anyway).  Actually we need a bound that works for all such \(N\).  A simpler approach: we can directly compute \(f(N)\) for \(N=2m\) and then show it is less than \(N\) by comparing the expression.  However, we can also prove Lemma 1 by a different method: for any \(N\) with \(\tau(N)\ge 4\), we have
\[
f(N) \le \frac{N}{2}+\frac{N}{3}+\frac{N}{4} \quad\text{or something? That's not always true.}
\]

Let's reconsider Lemma 1.  The claim is: if \(N\in T\) and \(N\) is not a multiple of \(6\), then \(f(N)<N\).  This is a key step.  We already gave a proof for odd \(N\).  For even \(N\) with \(3\nmid N\), we need to prove \(f(N)<N\).  Let's do it carefully.

We have \(N=2^e m\), \(m\) odd, \(e\ge 1\), \(3\nmid m\).  The three smallest divisors larger than \(1\) are: \(d_1=2\), \(d_2\) is the second smallest divisor.  Since \(3\nmid N\), the smallest odd divisor \(q\) of \(N\) is at least \(5\).  But is it possible that \(d_2=4\)?  Yes, if \(e\ge 2\) then \(4\mid N\) and \(4\) is a divisor.  Then \(d_2=4\).  If \(e=1\) then \(N=2m\) and the divisors larger than \(1\) are \(2\) and the odd divisors of \(m\).  The smallest odd divisor is \(q\ge 5\).  So \(d_2 = q\).  Now, \(d_3\) is the next smallest.  We have two subcases.

- **Subcase \(e\ge 2\):** then \(d_1=2,\; d_2=4\).  The third smallest divisor could be either \(8\) (if \(e\ge 3\)) or some odd divisor \(r\) (if \(m\) has a divisor less than \(8\) that is not \(1\) and not \(2\) or \(4\), but note that odd divisors are at least \(5\) and could be \(5\) or \(7\)).  However, \(2q\) is a divisor, and \(q\ge 5\).  So the third smallest is \(\min(8, q, 2q, \dots)\).  Since \(q\ge 5\) and \(2q\ge 10\), \(8\) could be smaller than \(q\) if \(q>8\).  But the maximum possible sum of reciprocals (to bound \(f(N)/N\)) is achieved when the three divisors are as small as possible.  The smallest possible values for \(d_1,d_2,d_3\) under the constraint that \(N\) is even and not a multiple of \(3\) are: \(d_1=2,\; d_2=4\) (since \(4\) is a divisor).  Then the third smallest could be \(5\) (if \(N\) is divisible by \(5\)) or \(8\) (if \(N\) is a power of \(2\)).  So the maximum of \(\sum 1/d_i\) is \(\frac12+\frac14+\frac15 = \frac{19}{20} < 1\).  Thus \(f(N)/N \le 19/20 < 1\).  Hence \(f(N)<N\).

- **Subcase \(e=1\):** then \(N=2m\), \(m\) odd, \(3\nmid m\).  The divisors larger than \(1\) are \(2\) and the odd divisors of \(m\).  The smallest odd divisor is \(q\ge 5\).  The third smallest divisor is either \(2q\) (if no odd divisor between \(q\) and \(2q\) exists) or some odd divisor \(r\) with \(q<r<2q\).  In the latter case, \(r\) is a prime (since any composite odd divisor would be at least \(q^2\), and \(q^2 > 2q\) for \(q\ge5\)).  So \(r\) is a prime \(p\) with \(q<p<2q\).  Then the three smallest divisors larger than \(1\) are \(2, q, p\).  Then
\[
\frac{1}{d_1}+\frac{1}{d_2}+\frac{1}{d_3} = \frac12+\frac1q+\frac1p.
\]
Now, note that \(q\ge 5\) and \(p\ge 7\).  The sum \(\frac12+\frac1q+\frac1p\) is at most \(\frac12+\frac15+\frac17 = \frac{71}{105} < 1\).  If instead the third smallest is \(2q\), then the reciprocals are \(\frac12+\frac1q+\frac1{2q} = \frac12+\frac{3}{2q}\).  Since \(q\ge5\), this is at most \(\frac12+\frac{3}{10} = \frac{8}{10} = \frac45 < 1\).  So in all cases \(f(N)/N < 1\).  Therefore \(f(N)<N\).

Thus Lemma 1 holds.

**Lemma 2.**  If \(N\in T\) and \(N\) is **not** a multiple of \(6\), then \(f(N)\) is **not** a multiple of \(6\).

*Proof.*  We again consider two cases.

*Case 1: \(N\) is odd.*  Then all divisors of \(N\) are odd, so each term \(N/d_i\) is odd.  Hence \(f(N)\) is odd.  Any multiple of \(6\) is even, so \(f(N)\) cannot be a multiple of \(6\).

*Case 2: \(N\) is even but \(3\nmid N\).*  Write \(N=2^e m\) with \(m\) odd, \(e\ge 1\) and \(3\nmid m\).  We analyse the three sub‑cases.

- **Subcase 2a:** \(e\ge 2\).  Then \(4\mid N\) and the three smallest divisors larger than \(1\) are \(2,4,d_3\) with \(d_3\ge 5\).  Consequently
  \[
  f(N)=\frac{N}{2}+\frac{N}{4}+\frac{N}{d_3} = \frac{3N}{4}+\frac{N}{d_3}.
  \]
  The term \(\frac{3N}{4}\) is an integer.  If \(f(N)\) were a multiple of \(6\), in particular it would be divisible by \(3\).  But modulo \(3\) we have
  \[
  f(N) \equiv \frac{N}{d_3} \pmod 3
  \]
  because \(\frac{3N}{4}\equiv 0\pmod 3\).  Since \(N\) is not a multiple of \(3\), none of its divisors are multiples of \(3\); thus \(\frac{N}{d_3}\) is not a multiple of \(3\).  Hence \(f(N)\) is not divisible by \(3\) and therefore cannot be a multiple of \(6\).

- **Subcase 2b:** \(e=1\).  Then \(N=2m\) with \(m\) odd, \(3\nmid m\).  Let \(q\) be the smallest odd prime factor of \(m\); then \(q\ge 5\).  The three smallest divisors larger than \(1\) of \(N\) are \(2\), \(q\), and some \(d_3\).

  *If \(m\) is a prime power \(q^a\) (\(a\ge 1\)):* then the only odd divisors of \(N\) are powers of \(q\).  Since \(q^2 > 2q\) for \(q\ge5\), the third smallest divisor is \(2q\) (it is a divisor because \(q\mid m\) and \(2q\mid 2m\)).  Thus
  \[
  f(N)=\frac{N}{2}+\frac{N}{q}+\frac{N}{2q}=m+\frac{2m}{q}+\frac{m}{q}=m+3\cdot\frac{m}{q}.
  \]
  Let \(a=m/q\) (which is an integer and is not divisible by \(3\) because \(m\) is not).  Then \(f(N)=a(q+3)\).  Since \(a\) and \(q\) are not multiples of \(3\), \(a\cdot q\) is not a multiple of \(3\); therefore \(f(N)\equiv a q\pmod 3\) is non‑zero, so \(f(N)\) is not divisible by \(3\).  In particular it is not a multiple of \(6\).

  *If \(m\) is **not** a prime power:* then \(m\) possesses at least two distinct odd prime factors.  By Bertrand’s postulate there exists a prime \(p\) with \(q<p<2q\); such a prime may or may not divide \(m\).  However, if no such prime divides \(m\), then the next smallest divisor after \(q\) is \(2q\) (because \(2q\) is a divisor and \(2q<q^2\), and any other odd divisor would be at least \(q^2\)).  In that situation the third smallest divisor is \(2q\) and we are in the same situation as the prime‑power case, so \(f(N)\) is not divisible by \(3\).  If, on the other hand, \(m\) does have a prime divisor \(p\) with \(q<p<2q\), then the three smallest divisors larger than \(1\) are \(2,q,p\).  Then
  \[
  f(N)=\frac{N}{2}+\frac{N}{q}+\frac{N}{p}=m+\frac{2m}{q}+\frac{2m}{p}.
  \]
  Since \(m = q\,t = p\,s\) for some integers \(t,s\) (because \(q\) and \(p\) divide \(m\)), we have \(t = p\,u\) and \(s = q\,u\) for some positive integer \(u\) (this is a consequence of the fact that \(m\) is divisible by \(q\) and \(p\) and that \(q\) and \(p\) are distinct primes).  Hence \(m = q\,p\,u\) and \(u\) is odd (because \(m\) is odd).  Then
  \[
  f(N)= qpu + \frac{2pu}{q} \cdot q? Wait compute: m = q p u.  Then
  \frac{2m}{q}=2pu,\quad \frac{2m}{p}=2qu.
  \]
  So
  \[
  f(N)= qpu + 2pu + 2qu = u\,(qp+2q+2p).
  \]
  The factor \(u\) is odd, and \(qp+2q+2p\) is odd because \(qp\) is odd and \(2q+2p\) is even.  Therefore \(f(N)\) is odd.  Consequently it cannot be a multiple of \(6\) (which would be even).

Thus in every sub‑case \(f(N)\) is not a multiple of \(6\).  This completes the proof of Lemma 2.

---

### 2.  Necessity:  \(a_1\) must be a multiple of \(6\)

Assume that the sequence exists (i.e. every term has at least three proper divisors).  We shall prove that \(a_1\) is a multiple of \(6\).  Suppose, to the contrary, that \(a_1\) is **not** a multiple of \(6\).  Then by Lemma 1 we have \(a_2 = f(a_1) < a_1\).  Because \(a_1\in T\), Lemma 2 tells us that \(a_2\) is **not** a multiple of \(6\).  Hence \(a_2\) is also not a multiple of \(6\).  Applying Lemma 1 to \(a_2\) (it belongs to \(T\) by hypothesis) yields \(a_3 = f(a_2) < a_2\) and, by Lemma 2, \(a_3\) is not a multiple of \(6\).  Proceeding by induction we obtain an infinite strictly decreasing sequence of positive integers
\[
a_1 > a_2 > a_3 > \cdots \ge 1,
\]
which is impossible.  Therefore our supposition was false, and \(a_1\) must be a multiple of \(6\).

---

### 3.  Sufficiency and characterisation of all possible \(a_1\)

We now determine all multiples of \(6\) that can be taken as \(a_1\) while keeping the sequence inside \(T\).  Let \(N\) be a multiple of \(6\).  Write \(N = 2^e \cdot 3^f \cdot t\) with \(t\) not divisible by \(2\) or \(3\).  We need to examine the orbit.

Recall the expression for \(f(N)\) when \(N\) has at least three proper divisors (which it does).  The three smallest divisors larger than \(1\) are:

* \(d_1 = 2\);
* \(d_2 = 3\);
* \(d_3\) is the minimum among the remaining divisors, which can be \(4\) (if \(e\ge 2\)), \(5\) (if \(5\mid t\)), \(6\) (if neither \(4\) nor \(5\) divides \(N\)), or possibly \(8,9,\dots\) but those are larger.

Thus we have three possibilities:

1. **\(N\) is not divisible by \(4\) nor by \(5\).**  Then \(d_3 = 6\) and
   \[
   f(N) = \frac{N}{2} + \frac{N}{3} + \frac{N}{6} = N.
   \]
   Hence \(N\) is a fixed point.  Consequently the sequence is constant, so it stays in \(T\) (as \(N\in T\)).

2. **\(N\) is divisible by \(4\) (i.e. \(e\ge 2\)), but not by \(5\).**  Then \(d_3 = 4\) and
   \[
   f(N) = \frac{N}{2} + \frac{N}{3} + \frac{N}{4} = \frac{13N}{12}.
   \]
   Hence \(a_2 = \frac{13N}{12}\).

3. **\(N\) is divisible by \(5\) (i.e. \(5\mid t\)), but not by \(4\).**  Then \(d_3 = 5\) and
   \[
   f(N) = \frac{N}{2} + \frac{N}{3} + \frac{N}{5} = \frac{31N}{30}.
   \]
   Hence \(a_2 = \frac{31N}{30}\).

4. **\(N\) is divisible by \(4\) and by \(5\).**  Then \(d_3 = 4\) (because \(4<5\)) and we are in case 2, with \(a_2 = \frac{13N}{12}\).

Now we examine which of these initial values can yield an infinite sequence with all terms in \(T\).

**First**, if \(N\) is a fixed point (case 1), then the sequence is constant and works.  So all numbers \(N\) that are multiples of \(6\) and are **not** divisible by \(4\) nor by \(5\) are admissible.

**Second**, suppose \(N\) is such that \(a_2 = \frac{13N}{12}\) (cases 2 or 4).  Then \(N\) is a multiple of \(12\) (because it is divisible by \(4\) and by \(3\)).  Write \(N = 12k\).  Then \(a_2 = 13k\).  For the sequence to continue, \(a_2\) must be in \(T\).  Moreover we need that the subsequent iterates stay in \(T\).  We already know from Lemma 1 and Lemma 2 that if \(a_2\) is **not** a multiple of \(6\), then the sequence would eventually be strictly decreasing and cannot be infinite.  Thus, for the orbit to be infinite, \(a_2\) must be a multiple of \(6\).  So we require \(13k\) to be a multiple of \(6\).  Since \(\gcd(13,6)=1\), this forces \(k\) to be a multiple of \(6\).  Hence \(k = 6u\) for some positive integer \(u\), and then \(N = 12\cdot 6u = 72u\).  Moreover, when \(k\) is a multiple of \(6\), the number \(a_2 = 13k = 13\cdot 6u = 78u\) is a multiple of \(6\).  Is it a fixed point?  We need to check whether the orbit from that point can be infinite.  We know that if \(a_2\) is a multiple of \(6\) and is not a fixed point, then the sequence might still be infinite, but we have to examine.
